achieve a higher stability at high potentials but the activity is not better than Pt at 0.9 V, whilst a high Mo content (above 30%, e.g.PtMo) will start to destabilize the alloy and also reduce the activity.\"", "English": "The stability of Pt-based alloys, such as Pt-3d alloys, is influenced by the oxygen coverage under electrochemical conditions. Alloys are generally less stable than pure Pt at the same oxygen coverage. Maintaining zero oxygen coverage at working potentials is crucial for achieving both high activity and stability. The stability of clean alloy surfaces can be inferred from the calculated Pt d-band structure, which shows that alloying generally leads to the stabilization of the Pt d-band, making surface Pt atoms more difficult to segregate or dissolve. However, in the presence of adsorbed O atoms, the removal of Pt is facilitated because the surface vacancy can be more strongly stabilized by O atoms. This is due to the fact that Pt atoms at the surface vacancy are less coordinated and possess more active d-states, which can more strongly bond with O atoms compared to terrace Pt atoms. Additionally, with the exposure of the second layer atoms at the surface vacancy, it is possible to form a much stronger OM bond compared to the OPt bond. This is observed in the search for the optimum structure after a Pt removal, which shows that the surface vacancy is preferentially terminated by O atoms that sink to bond with the solute M atoms at the second layer. For example, in Pt2Ti skin alloys, the surface vacancies with a configuration containing OTi bonding are much more stable than those with only OPt bonding. This is consistent with the fact that atomic O on metals is anion-like, with a strong tendency to accept electrons, and prefers to form ionic-bonds. The stability of Pt alloys under electrochemical conditions is generally lower than that of pure Pt when the oxygen coverage is the same, and maintaining zero oxygen coverage at working potentials (such as 0.9 V) is crucial for achieving both high activity and stability. Among more than 20 different Pt2M alloys, molybdenum (Mo) is identified as a good element that can alloy with Pt to form a stable surface at high electrochemical potentials. Pt2Mo has a lower kinetic barrier for oxygen reduction (OR) compared to Pt, and the barrier to OOH dissociation is a key kinetic parameter for OR activity. The higher stability of clean alloy surfaces can indeed be inferred from the calculated Pt d-band structure, which shows that alloying generally leads to the stabilization of the Pt d-band, making surface Pt atoms more difficult to segregate or dissolve. The stability of surfaces follows the order Pt2Mo > Pt > Pt2Ag > Pt2Pd > Pt2Nb > Pt2Ti > Pt2Sc > Pt2Zr, and only Pt2Mo exhibits higher stability than pure Pt at high potential (1.05 V). The oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) on a Pt2Mo alloy surface follows a similar reaction profile as on Pt, with the transition state (TS) for OOH dissociation determining the height of the potential profile. The TSs for OOH dissociation and O2 dissociation dictate the highest free energy positions in the reaction pathways. The aqueous environment, together with the in situ oxygen coverage, plays an important role in facilitating the ORR on alloy surfaces. The stability of PtxMo alloys drops quickly with increasing potential, in line with the rapid increase of surface oxygen coverage for PtxMo surfaces above 0.9 V. The Pt2Mo skin alloy achieves the best balance of stability and activity. A low Mo content (below 30%, e.g., Pt5Mo) may achieve a higher stability at high potentials, but the activity is not better than Pt at 0.9 V, while a high Mo content (above 30%, e.g., PtMo) will start to destabilize the alloy and also reduce the activity.", "Doi": "10.1039/c0ee00762e"},
{"Quotes": "Adsorption-Driven Surface Segregation of the Less Reactive Alloy Component Klas J. AnderssonFederico Calle-VallejoJan RossmeislIb Chorkendorff* View Author Information View Author Information Open PDF Abstract Counterintuitive to expectations and all prior observations of adsorbate-induced surface segregation of the more reactive alloy component (the one forming the stronger bond with the adsorbate), we show that CO adsorption at elevated pressures and temperatures pulls the less reactive Cu to the surface of a CuPt near-surface alloy.\nThe Cu surface segregation is driven by the formation of a stable self-organized CO/CuPt surface alloy structure and is rationalized in terms of the radically stronger PtCO bond when Cu is present in the first surface layer of Pt.\nThe results, which are expected to apply to a range of coinage (Cu, Ag)/Pt-group bimetallic surface alloys, open up new possibilities in selective and dynamical engineering of alloy surfaces for catalysis.\nThe bonding of adsorbates may induce changes in local atomic composition and surface structure, changing the activity and selectivity of the catalyst.\nThe CuPt NSA was recently suggested as a promising water-gas shift (CO + H2O  H2 + CO2) catalyst because of reduced PtCO bonding on a Pt catalyst surface with Cu present in the second layer, (15) alleviating possible CO-poisoning problems.\nThe results are confirmed by extended DFT calculations.\nAlthough Cu diffusion to the first layer is endothermic, (15) the observed drastic increase in CO binding energy more than compensates for the endothermicity.\nThe calculations also show weakening of the PtCO bond with increasing Cu content in the second layer.\nThe results with adsorbed CO in Table 1 can explain the θCu > 2/3 ML onset of bulk migration of Cu indicated by our XPS data.\nIn comparison with the θCu = 2/3 ML case, situations with higher Cu content in the top two layers are significantly less stable, leading to a significant Cu bulk migration driving force.\nThe interdiffusion of Cu and Pt becomes significant at 460 K, (17) and this is also approximately where both the formation and breakup of the stable CO-induced surface alloy start to occur (see Figure 2).\nIn forming the SA from the NSA, the interdiffusion mobility allows for Cu migration events to the first layer, and this Cu can then be trapped in the new low-energy SA configuration with CO adsorbed.\nDFT calculations reproduce the experimental observations and confirm that the vastly increased PtCO binding energy with 2/3 ML of Cu in the first layer of Pt rationalizes the observed Cu surface segregation.\nAn important consequence of our results is that they add to the promise of using molecular adsorbate-induced surface changes as a tool for selective and dynamical engineering of alloy surfaces.\nIn view of the earlier indications of increased Pt-group metal bonding to CO when Cu or Ag is present in the first layer with a Pt-group metal, (19-22) our results for the CuPt system at elevated temperatures and CO pressures, where a novel stable CO-induced surface alloy forms, likely extend to a wide variety of coinage (Cu, Ag)/Pt-group bimetallic surface alloys.\nCounterintuitive to expectations and all prior This is rationalized by the significantly stronger PtCO bond when Cu is present in the first surface layer of Pt. The results suggest new possibilities for selective and dynamical engineering of alloy surfaces for catalysis, with implications for catalytic properties and performance in various applications, including water-gas shift reactions. This is due to the drastically stronger PtCO bond when Cu is present in the first surface layer of Pt. The bonding of adsorbates can induce changes in local atomic composition and surface structure, changing the activity and selectivity of the catalyst. The CuPt near-surface alloy (NSA) was suggested as a promising water-gas shift catalyst because of reduced PtCO bonding on a Pt catalyst surface with Cu present in the second layer. However, the NSA is unstable at elevated CO pressures and sample temperatures, forming instead a self-organized CO/CuPt surface alloy (SA) structure. The interdiffusion of Cu and Pt becomes significant at 460 K, and the formation and breakup of the stable CO-induced surface alloy start to occur at this temperature. The DFT calculations reproduce the experimental observations and confirm the rationalization of the observed Cu surface segregation. {"Quotes": "Thermodynamics and kinetics of oxygen-induced segregation of 3 d metals in Pt\n          DFT calculations of Pt3dPt (111) and Pt3dPt (100)\n          Predicted stability of subsurface 3d metal in Pt(111) and Pt(100) in oxygen\n          Effect of crystal plane on subsurface stability\n          Predicted effect of crystal plane on ORR activity", "English": "The thermodynamics and kinetics of oxygen-induced segregation of 3d metals in Pt-based systems, such as Pt-3d alloys, are influenced by factors including the crystal plane, which affects the stability of subsurface 3d metals. This has implications for the catalytic properties and performance of bimetallic catalysts in various applications, particularly in oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) activity. The stability of subsurface 3d metals in Pt(111) and Pt(100) under oxygen exposure is predicted through Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculations. The crystal plane has an effect on the subsurface stability of the bimetallic catalysts. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Compared with monometallic catalysts, bimetallic catalysts have more surface structural complexity, where the second metalin either the metallic or the oxidized statecan be exposed on the surface,in the near surface region, or embedded in bulk region. It has been demonstrated that the surface structure of the bimetallic catalyst is critical to their catalytic properties.\"\n          [2] \"For example, Pt- based bimetallic catalysts consisting of Pt-skin surfaces with 3d transition metals at the subsurface region present super catalytic performances in many reactions compared to the pure Pt surface.\"\n          [3] \"The structure of a catalyst surface often changes dynamically during reactions. For example, reversible surface processes such as wettingdewetting and sintering dispersion have been observed in monometallic catalyst sys- tems when the gaseous environment changes between reduc- tive and oxidative conditions.\"\n          [4] \"The bimetallic catalysts may show similar dependence of surface structure on the redox po- tential of reaction gases, which offers eventually the opportu- nity to tune the catalytic activity and selectivity.\"", "English": "The thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions significantly influence the stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys. Bimetallic catalysts exhibit greater surface structural complexity compared to monometallic catalysts, with the second metal, in either its metallic or oxidized state, being present on the surface, in the near-surface region, or embedded in the bulk region. The surface structure of bimetallic catalysts is crucial to their catalytic properties. For instance, Pt-based bimetallic catalysts with Pt-skin surfaces and 3d transition metals in the subsurface region demonstrate superior catalytic performance in many reactions compared to pure Pt surfaces. The structure of the catalyst surface often changes dynamically during reactions, similar to reversible surface processes observed in monometallic catalyst systems when the gaseous environment shifts between reductive and oxidative conditions. Bimetallic catalysts may exhibit a similar dependence of surface structure on the redox potential of reaction gases, providing an opportunity to adjust catalytic activity and selectivity.", "Doi": "10.1002/cphc.200900053"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"One of the most critical questions in bimetallic catalysis is the stability of bimetallic surface structures under reaction conditions, which would in turn modify the catalytic properties.\"\n          [2] \"the monolayer surface Ni atoms diffuse into the subsurface region of Pt(111) upon annealing, indicating that the subsurface PtNiPt(111) structure is thermodynamically more stable than the surface NiPtPt(111) structure in vacuum, i.e., without adsorbates.\"\n          [3] \"Several other investigations of Pt3d alloys in fuel cell applications have suggested that the alloy films used in these studies are covered by a Pt skin, (195-197) again indicating the thermodynamic preference of Pt-terminated bimetallic surfaces.\"\n          [4] \"If Eseg is sufficiently negative, the admetal segregates to the surface to produce the MHH surface monolayer structure. If Eseg is sufficiently positive, the surface layer is the host metal, leading to the formation of the HMH subsurface monolayer structure.\"\n          [5] \"the thermodynamic driving force of hydrogen-induced segregation was related to two terms: the value of Eseg and the difference between the hydrogen-binding energies on the surface and subsurface structures.\"\n          [6] \"Because hydrogen binds relatively weakly to most transition metals, only a few bimetallic surfaces experience a hydrogen-induced segregation of the subsurface admetals.\"\n          [7] \"the subsurface configuration remains thermodynamically preferred with adsorbed hydrogen. However, the subsurface structure is no longer stable in the presence of 0.5 ML atomic oxygen, indicating a thermodynamic driving potential for the subsurface 3d metals to segregate to the surface.\"\n          [8] \"An adsorbate with a weak Pauling electronegativity, such as H, S, Se, or C, binds weakly with both surface and subsurface configurations and provides only a small shift in the thermodynamic potential for interchanging the configuration.\"\n          [9] \"An adsorbate with a strong Pauling electronegativity, such as N or O, produces a large difference in binding energy between the two configurations. When the surface coverage of N or O is high enough, this difference outweighs the thermodynamic potential for retaining the subsurface configuration in vacuum.\"\n          [10] \"The oxygen-induced surface segregation is also predicted for the (211) surfaces of Pt(shell)/M(core) and Pt3M (M = Co, Ir) alloys by Callejas-Tovar and Balbuena.\"", "English": "The stability of bimetallic surface structures under reaction conditions is a critical factor in bimetallic catalysis, as it can modify catalytic properties. For example, under annealing conditions, the subsurface PtNiPt(111) structure is more thermodynamically stable than the surface NiPtPt(111) structure in vacuum. Pt3d alloys in fuel cell applications often have a Pt skin, indicating a thermodynamic preference for Pt-terminated bimetallic surfaces. The segregation energy (Eseg) determines whether the admetal segregates to the surface or remains in the subsurface, affecting the surface composition. Hydrogen-induced segregation is influenced by Eseg and the difference in hydrogen-binding energies on the surface and subsurface. Oxygen can destabilize the subsurface structure, driving subsurface 3d metals to segregate to the surface. Adsorbates with weak Pauling electronegativity have a small effect on the thermodynamic potential for interchanging surface and subsurface configurations, while those with strong Pauling electronegativity can significantly alter binding energy, leading to surface segregation when their coverage is high enough. Oxygen-induced surface segregation is also predicted for specific Pt-based alloy surfaces.", "Doi": "10.1021/cr300096b_5"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Under reductive conditions (vacuum and CO), 3d TM prefers to occupy in the subsurface layer due to its higher surface energy and smaller atomic size.\"\n          [2] \"While under the oxidizing conditions, the strong O3d TMs interaction would induce 3d TM segregation to the surface, migrating further in the surface layer to form oxidized 3d TM islands.\"\n          [3] \"We find that Pt (1 1 1) with the atomic dispersed 3d TM in surface layers is highly active for CO oxidation via bi-functional mechanism.\"\n          [4] \"The interplay between structure and reaction environment as well as effect on the catalytic activity is highlighted.\"", "English": "The thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions significantly influence the stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys. Under reductive conditions, such as vacuum and carbon monoxide, 3d transition metals prefer to occupy the subsurface layer due to their higher surface energy and smaller atomic size. However, under oxidizing conditions, the strong interaction between oxygen and 3d transition metals induces their segregation to the surface, leading to the formation of oxidized 3d transition metal islands. These structural changes have profound implications for the catalytic properties and performance of these materials in various applications. For instance, Pt(111) surfaces with atomic dispersed 3d transition metals in the surface layers exhibit high activity for CO oxidation through a bi-functional mechanism. The interplay between the structure, reaction environment, and catalytic activity is a critical factor in determining the performance of alloy catalysts.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.cattod.2010.10.069"},

bimetallic catalyst systems are of great significance in many real catalytic processes.\"", "English": "The surface structures of PtNi, PtCo, and PtCu bimetallic catalysts exhibit reversible changes in response to the redox potential of the environmental gas. These changes can be controlled by treating the catalysts in various gases. Surface segregation is a common phenomenon in bimetallic surfaces, where a metal with a larger atomic radius tends to segregate to the surface to release strain. Platinum, with its larger atomic radius compared to cobalt, nickel, and copper, is more likely to segregate to the surface. The presence of hydrogen does not alter the thermodynamic driving force for surface segregation. Oxygen adsorption on Pt-M catalyst surfaces can induce the segregation of 3d transition metals to the top surface due to the strong bonding between oxygen and 3d transition metals. In oxidative environments, 3d transition metals can become oxidized, and the formed oxides tend to encapsulate platinum surfaces. The mass transport processes that occur during oxidation or reduction reactions are fully reversible, depending on the redox potential of the reaction gases. The reversible structural changes in Pt-based bimetallic catalysts have significant implications for real catalytic processes.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.apsusc.2009.03.086"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Fe-Pt catalysts have also been used in the preferential oxidation (PROX) of CO in excess H2, and a high selectivity of approximately 100% for CO oxidation was ob- MA Teng et al. / Chinese Journal of Catalysis, 2010, 31: 2432\"\n          [2] \"The Pt-skin structures also showed high activity for catalyzing the hydrogenation of unsaturated hydrocarbons [13].\"\n          [3] \"The unique catalytic properties of the Pt-skin surfaces were attributed to the modification of the electronic structure of the surface Pt by the subsurface Fe underneath, which influenced the adsorption and reaction characteristics of the surface species [14,15].\"\n          [4] \"The controlled preparation of the various Fe-Pt surface structures enabled us to derive the rela- tionship between the structure and catalytic properties, which would help the design of high-performance and low-cost catalysts.\"", "English": "The stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys, are influenced by thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions. Fe-Pt catalysts have been used in the preferential oxidation (PROX) of CO in excess H2, achieving high selectivity for CO oxidation. Pt-skin structures show high activity for catalyzing the hydrogenation of unsaturated hydrocarbons. The unique catalytic properties of the Pt-skin surfaces are attributed to the modification of the electronic structure of the surface Pt by the subsurface Fe underneath, influencing the adsorption and reaction characteristics of the surface species. The controlled preparation of various Fe-Pt surface structures allows for the derivation of the relationship between structure and catalytic properties, aiding in the design of high-performance and low-cost catalysts. The stability of the Pt-skin structure can be reduced under certain circumstances, such as an oxidative environment, leading to gas-induced Fe surface segregation. The structural configuration of the catalyst can be transformed between surface Fe or FeO and subsurface Fe on the Pt(111) surface by cycles of oxidation and reduction. These transformations have implications for the catalytic properties and performance of the catalysts in various applications, including oxygen reduction reactions, hydrogenation of unsaturated hydrocarbons, and the preferential oxidation of CO in excess H2. Depending on the preparation procedures and reaction conditions, Fe overlayers with either metallic or oxidized states may be present as the topmost surface or in the subsurface region of Pt. Different reactions can produce different Fe-Pt surface structural configurations. For oxygen reduction reactions (ORR), Pt-skin structures with Fe or some other transition metals in the subsurface regions showed a higher activity than pure Pt. For instance, Fe49Pt51 alloys with 100% Pt as the topmost layer had 25 times the activity of pure Pt catalysts in ORR. The optimization of the performance of Fe-Pt structures in magnetism and catalysis places a high demand on the design and control of the surface structural configurations and needs information on the stability of the various structures at elevated temperatures and under different reaction atmospheres.", "Doi": "10.1016/s1872-2067(09)60037-1"},

"The stability of Pt3dPt subsurface structures in various conditions is a critical factor in their catalytic properties and performance. These structures are desirable in heterogeneous catalysis and electrocatalysis. DFT predictions indicate that these structures are stable in vacuum and with adsorbed hydrogen, but with adsorbed oxygen, the 3d atoms should segregate to the surface. These predictions have been experimentally verified on selected Pt3dPt(111) systems and expanded to polycrystalline Pt to understand the effects of multiple crystal facets on the kinetic barriers for the segregation of subsurface 3d atoms. The Pt3dPt subsurface structures are predicted to have higher activation barriers for oxygen-induced segregation compared to the respective Pt3dPt(111) configuration. The PtNiPt system is the most resistant to oxygen-induced segregation, while the PtTiPt configuration is the most susceptible. The stability of these structures in vacuum and with adsorbed hydrogen and oxygen has been demonstrated through DFT calculations and experimental verification on both single crystal Pt(111) and polycrystalline Pt foil. Using combined theoretical and experimental approaches, the relative stability of surface and subsurface bimetallic structures can be quantified and general trends predicted. The difference in the occupied d-band center, Δεd, can be used to screen potential bimetallic alloys with desirable stability in different environments. A BrnstedEvansPolanyi relationship has been derived to correlate thermodynamic stability from DFT calculations with kinetic barriers from experimental measurements for the Pt3dPt systems. Empirical equations have been developed to predict the thermodynamic stability of other bimetallic systems in various conditions. The large difference in binding energies between surface and subsurface structures highlights the need to understand their stability in reacting environments. For Pt3d bimetallic systems, the difference in oxygen binding energy for these configurations is on the order of hundreds of kcal/mol. Based on Sabatier's principle, if a subsurface alloy structure exhibits optimal binding properties for dehydrogenation and ORR, the surface structure would likely bind intermediates too strongly, halting the reaction. Conversely, if the surface alloy structure exhibits optimal binding properties for steam reforming, the subsurface structure would bind adsorbates too weakly, preventing the reaction from proceeding. The stability of bimetallic structures has been considered with various atomic species, which can be extended to more complex adsorbates using scaling relationships. DFT results predict that subsurface PtMPt(111) configurations are preferred in vacuum and with 0.5 ML H, while surface MPtPt(111) configurations are preferred with 0.5 ML O. Experimental measurements have verified the predicted segregation of subsurface 3d atoms in the presence of oxygen and quantified the activation barrier for adsorbate-induced segregation. AES results suggest two regions of segregation, revealing that DFT predictions and experimental measurements on single crystal bimetallic surfaces are applicable to polycrystalline substrates, bridging the materials gap. Predictions show that all Pt3dPt(100) structures have a higher activation barrier for oxygen-induced segregation compared to respective Pt3dPt(111) configurations. Linear trends in all systems show that the correlation of thermodynamic stability with the difference in d-band center can be used beyond Pt3d alloys. Switching the admetal/host component shifts the absolute driving potential of the segregation. This general stability trend predicts that all bimetallic systems have the same thermodynamically preferred configuration in each environment, with subsurface configurations preferred in vacuum and with 0.5 ML H, and surface configurations favored with 0.5 ML O.", "Doi": "10.1007/s11244-010-9447-2"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Platinum has become the most popular cathode electrocatalyst in polymer electrode membrane (PEM) fuel cells. However, its relatively sluggish kinetics for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) significantly limits the catalytic efficiency.\"\n          [2] \"In recent years, Pt-based alloys, composed of Pt and other transition metals, have been extensively investigated (1-7) owing to their enhanced ORR activity.\"\n          [3] \"Surface segregation has been found to play a key role in the process.\"\n          [4] \"A pure Pt-skin is formed in the outmost layer of the alloys due to surface segregation of Pt atoms.\"\n          [5] \"The enhanced stability of Pt-based catalysts is also closely related to surface segregation.\"\n          [6] \"Au shows strong surface segregation in PtAu alloys, (29, 30) whereas Co is usually unable to enrich PtCo alloy surfaces. (16, 17)\"\n          [7] \"The pathway from 1 to 4 describes a complete cycle in which an impurity atom in the second layer segregates to the Pt(111) surface. The process is exothermic (0.57 eV) for the PtAu system, whereas it is endothermic (0.45 eV) for the PtCo system.\"\n          [8] \"This leads to the conclusion that Au surface segregation is thermodynamically favorable, consistent with previous studies. (26, 28-30)\"\n          [9] \"For the PtCo alloy, the conversion from 2 to 1 has an energy barrier of 0.63 eV, much lower than that of the competitive step from 3 to 4 (1.15 eV), suggesting the surface Co atoms have a stronger tendency to migrate to the second layer compared to the Pt atoms.\"\n          [10] \"Accordingly, structure 4 most likely will not be formed, and thus the continuous segregation of Co cannot be reached.\"\n          [11] \"Therefore, Co segregating to the clean Pt(111) surface is both thermodynamically unfavorable and kinetically prohibitive according to our model.\"\n          [12] \"Surface segregation has been found to play a key role in the process. For example, Stamenkovic et al. compared the kinetics of the ORR on bulk Pt3Co alloys and on Pt-skin structures produced by Pt segregating to the outmost layer of the annealed alloy surface. They noted that the ORR is uniquely active on Pt-skin surfaces and the reaction rate is four times that of the pure Pt catalyst.\"\n          [13] \"Owing to the importance of surface segregation for Pt-based alloys, numerous studies have been carried out using modern experimental and computational techniques.\"\n          [14] \"For many Pt alloy systems, segregation trends have been unambiguously identified, and the components of surface and subsurface regions have successfully been characterized.\"\n          [15] \"The dissolution of metal atoms results in some defects on the alloy surface, which are critical to the rearrangement process.\"\n          [16] \"The phenomenon can also be observed in acidic solution, often with dissolution of the alloyed metal and rearrangement of Pt atoms.\"\n          [17] \"The segregation process thus includes interlayer migration of the impurity atom, vacancy movement at the second layer, and vacancy regeneration at the surface layer.\"\n          [18] \"Our segregation model can successfully interpret these phenomena, particularly, from a kinetics viewpoint.\"\n          [19] \"The pathway from 1 to 4 describes a complete cycle in which an impurity atom in the second layer segregates to the Pt(111) surface.\"\n          [20] \"The process is exothermic (0.57 eV) for the PtAu system, whereas it is endothermic (0.45 eV) for the PtCo system.\"\n          [21] \"Therefore, Co segregating to the clean Pt(111) surface is both thermodynamically unfavorable and kinetically prohibitive according to our model.\"", "English": "The stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys, are influenced by thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions. Platinum is a popular cathode electrocatalyst in PEM fuel cells, but its slow kinetics for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) limits catalytic efficiency. In recent years, Pt-based alloys, which include Pt and other transition metals, have been extensively studied due to their enhanced ORR activity. Surface segregation plays a key role in this process, leading to the formation of a pure Pt-skin in the outermost layer of the alloys. This surface segregation is closely related to the enhanced stability of Pt-based catalysts. Au shows strong surface segregation in PtAu alloys, whereas Co is usually unable to enrich PtCo alloy surfaces. The process of Au segregating from the second layer to the surface is exothermic, indicating that Au surface segregation is thermodynamically favorable. However, for the PtCo alloy, the surface Co atoms have a stronger tendency to migrate to the second layer, making the continuous segregation of Co unlikely. Therefore, Co segregating to the clean Pt(111) surface is both thermodynamically unfavorable and kinetically prohibitive. Surface segregation has been found to play a key role in the process of Pt-based alloys. For instance, Stamenkovic et al. found that the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) is uniquely active on Pt-skin surfaces, and the reaction rate is four times that of the pure Pt catalyst. Numerous studies have been carried out using modern experimental and computational techniques to understand the importance of surface segregation for Pt-based alloys. For many Pt alloy systems, segregation trends have been unambiguously identified, and the components of surface and subsurface regions have successfully been characterized. The dissolution of metal atoms results in some defects on the alloy surface, which are critical to the rearrangement process. The phenomenon can also be observed in acidic solution, often with dissolution of the alloyed metal and rearrangement of Pt atoms. The segregation process includes interlayer migration of the impurity atom, vacancy movement at the second layer, and vacancy regeneration at the surface layer. The segregation model can successfully interpret these phenomena, particularly from a kinetics viewpoint. The pathway from 1 to 4 describes a complete cycle in which an impurity atom in the second layer segregates to the Pt(111) surface. The process is exothermic for the PtAu system, whereas it is endothermic for the PtCo system. Therefore, Co segregating to the clean Pt(111) surface is both thermodynamically unfavorable and kinetically prohibitive according to the model.", "Doi": "10.1021/ct8004062"},
{"Quotes": "", "English": "The thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions significantly influence the stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys. The introduction of 3d metals into the Pt(111) system considerably alters the surface adsorption properties, which can be considerably modified by the 3d metals. The electronic structure of the surface Pt atoms only differs very slightly for the segregated and nonsegregated alloys. The discrepant adsorption properties are therefore mainly attributed to the direct involvement of 3d metals either on the surface or in the subsurface. The stability of Pt atoms of Pt-skin surfaces of the three alloys is enhanced with respect to those in pure Pt surfaces. Such stability enhancement is observed both in clean surfaces and under 0.25 ML of adsorbed atomic oxygen, and the degree of stability enhancement follows the opposite order of the oxygen adsorption strengths in the three studied alloys, indicating the protective role of the 3d subsurface atoms determined by their strong binding to the Pt surface layer. The presence of 3d metals in the alloys modifies the electronic and geometric properties, altering the binding strength of adsorbates. This can lead to enhanced catalytic properties and performance in various applications, such as increased oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) rate and improved electrochemical stability against dissolution. The formation of a Pt-skin layer on the surface of Pt-3d alloys, accompanied by a Pt-depleted layer underneath, is a result of surface segregation that can facilitate the removal of adsorbed species, increasing the ORR rate. The stability enhancement of Pt-skin surfaces is associated with a positive potential shift, indicating that the dissolution of Pt atoms is delayed to higher potentials. Surface segregation, where one metal species enriches the surface or the first sub-surface layers over other components, has significant implications for materials science and surface chemistry. It affects physical and chemical properties such as permeation, surface adsorption, crystal growth, and metal corrosion. Notably, the catalytic activities of alloys can be dramatically altered through surface segregation. Pt, an efficient catalyst used in important chemical reactions like methanol oxidation and hydrocarbon reforming, is the most widely used electrocatalyst for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) in fuel cells. Efforts to find alternative catalysts to Pt have focused on Pt-based bimetallic alloys, where some platinum is replaced by less expensive metals. The catalytic properties of these alloys are determined by the surface geometry and electronic structure, which can be modified by the chemical composition, leading to improved catalytic effects when appropriate structures are achieved. Enhanced ORR activities have been observed in Pt alloys with 3d metals, with surface segregation playing a key role. For example, Stamenkovic et al. found that the ORR on Pt-skin structures produced by Pt segregating on the surface of annealed Pt3Co alloys is uniquely active, with a reaction rate four times that of pure Pt. The enhanced activities can be attributed to the surface electronic structure and surface bond length of alloy systems. The surface geometry can considerably alter catalytic efficiency, but for Pt3Co (1 1 1) and Pt3Ni (1 1 1) systems, the maximum deviation of the surface bond length after Pt segregation is only 0.002 Å, indicating that the geometric effect does not contribute to the enhanced ORR activities of Pt-skin surfaces. Instead, the electronic structures modified through surface segregation are the main factor for such enhancement. Theoretical approaches can help to elucidate the driving force and the tendency of this phenomenon. Surface segregation is also governed by temperature and by the presence of adsorbates.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.susc.2007.09.052"},

Alloys are generally less stable than pure Pt at the same oxygen coverage. Maintaining zero oxygen coverage at working potentials is crucial for achieving both high activity and stability. The stability of clean alloy surfaces can be inferred from the calculated Pt d-band structure, which shows that alloying generally leads to the stabilization of the Pt d-band, making surface Pt atoms more difficult to segregate or dissolve. However, in the presence of adsorbed O atoms, the removal of Pt is facilitated because the surface vacancy can be more strongly stabilized by O atoms. This is due to the fact that Pt atoms at the surface vacancy are less coordinated and possess more active d-states, which can more strongly bond with O atoms compared to terrace Pt atoms. Additionally, with the exposure of the second layer atoms at the surface vacancy, it is possible to form a much stronger OM bond compared to the OPt bond. This is observed in the search for the optimum structure after a Pt removal, which shows that the surface vacancy is preferentially terminated by O atoms that sink to bond with the solute M atoms at the second layer. For example, in Pt2Ti skin alloys, the surface vacancies with a configuration containing OTi bonding are much more stable than those with only OPt bonding. This is consistent with the fact that atomic O on metals is anion-like, with a strong tendency to accept electrons, and prefers to form ionic-bonds. The stability of Pt alloys under electrochemical conditions is generally lower than that of pure Pt when the oxygen coverage is the same, and maintaining zero oxygen coverage at working potentials (such as 0.9 V) is crucial for achieving both high activity and stability. Among more than 20 different Pt2M alloys, molybdenum (Mo) is identified as a good element that can alloy with Pt to form a stable surface at high electrochemical potentials. Pt2Mo has a lower kinetic barrier for oxygen reduction (OR) compared to Pt, and the barrier to OOH dissociation is a key kinetic parameter for OR activity. The higher stability of clean alloy surfaces can indeed be inferred from the calculated Pt d-band structure, which shows that alloying generally leads to the stabilization of the Pt d-band, making surface Pt atoms more difficult to segregate or dissolve. The stability of surfaces follows the order Pt2Mo > Pt > Pt2Ag > Pt2Pd > Pt2Nb > Pt2Ti > Pt2Sc > Pt2Zr, and only Pt2Mo exhibits higher stability than pure Pt at high potential (1.05 V). The oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) on a Pt2Mo alloy surface follows a similar reaction profile as on Pt, with the transition state (TS) for OOH dissociation determining the height of the potential profile. The TSs for OOH dissociation and O2 dissociation dictate the highest free energy positions in the reaction pathways. The aqueous environment, together with the in situ oxygen coverage, plays an important role in facilitating the ORR on alloy surfaces. The stability of PtxMo alloys drops quickly with increasing potential, in line with the rapid increase of surface oxygen coverage for PtxMo surfaces above 0.9 V. The Pt2Mo skin alloy achieves the best balance of stability and activity. A low Mo content (below 30%, e.g., Pt5Mo) may achieve a higher stability at high potentials, but the activity is not better than Pt at 0.9 V, while a high Mo content (above 30%, e.g., PtMo) will start to destabilize the alloy and also reduce the activity.", "Doi": "10.1039/c0ee00762e"},
{"Quotes": "Adsorption-Driven Surface Segregation of the Less Reactive Alloy Component Klas J. AnderssonFederico Calle-VallejoJan RossmeislIb Chorkendorff* View Author Information View Author Information Open PDF Abstract Counterintuitive to expectations and all prior observations of adsorbate-induced surface segregation of the more reactive alloy component (the one forming the stronger bond with the adsorbate), we show that CO adsorption at elevated pressures and temperatures pulls the less reactive Cu to the surface of a CuPt near-surface alloy.\nThe Cu surface segregation is driven by the formation of a stable self-organized CO/CuPt surface alloy structure and is rationalized in terms of the radically stronger PtCO bond when Cu is present in the first surface layer of Pt.\nThe results, which are expected to apply to a range of coinage (Cu, Ag)/Pt-group bimetallic surface alloys, open up new possibilities in selective and dynamical engineering of alloy surfaces for catalysis.\nThe bonding of adsorbates may induce changes in local atomic composition and surface structure, changing the activity and selectivity of the catalyst.\nThe CuPt NSA was recently suggested as a promising water-gas shift (CO + H2O  H2 + CO2) catalyst because of reduced PtCO bonding on a Pt catalyst surface with Cu present in the second layer, (15) alleviating possible CO-poisoning problems.\nThe results are confirmed by extended DFT calculations.\nAlthough Cu diffusion to the first layer is endothermic, (15) the observed drastic increase in CO binding energy more than compensates for the endothermicity.\nThe calculations also show weakening of the PtCO bond with increasing Cu content in the second layer.\nThe results with adsorbed CO in Table 1 can explain the θCu > 2/3 ML onset of bulk migration of Cu indicated by our XPS data.\nIn comparison with the θCu = 2/3 ML case, situations with higher Cu content in the top two layers are significantly less stable, leading to a significant Cu bulk migration driving force.\nThe interdiffusion of Cu and Pt becomes significant at 460 K, (17) and this is also approximately where both the formation and breakup of the stable CO-induced surface alloy start to occur (see Figure 2).\nIn forming the SA from the NSA, the interdiffusion mobility allows for Cu migration events to the first layer, and this Cu can then be trapped in the new low-energy SA configuration with CO adsorbed.\nDFT calculations reproduce the experimental observations and confirm that the vastly increased PtCO binding energy with 2/3 ML of Cu in the first layer of Pt rationalizes the observed Cu surface segregation.\nAn important consequence of our results is that they add to the promise of using molecular adsorbate-induced surface changes as a tool for selective and dynamical engineering of alloy surfaces.\nIn view of the pressures, where a novel stable CO-induced surface alloy forms, likely extend to a wide variety of coinage (Cu, Ag)/Pt-group bimetallic surface alloys.\nCounterintuitive to expectations and all prior {"Quotes": "[1] \"Compared with monometallic catalysts, bimetallic catalysts have more surface structural complexity, where the second metalin either the metallic or the oxidized statecan be exposed on the surface,in the near surface region, or embedded in bulk region. {"Quotes": "[1] \"One of the most critical questions in bimetallic catalysis is the stability of bimetallic surface structures under reaction conditions, which would in turn modify the catalytic properties.\"\n          [2] \"the monolayer surface Ni atoms diffuse into the subsurface region of Pt(111) upon annealing, indicating that the subsurface PtNiPt(111) structure is thermodynamically more stable than the surface NiPtPt(111) structure in vacuum, i.e., without adsorbates.\"\n          [3] \"Several other investigations of Pt3d alloys in fuel cell applications have suggested that the alloy films used in these studies are covered by a Pt skin, (195-197) again indicating the thermodynamic preference of Pt-terminated bimetallic surfaces.\"\n          [4] \"If Eseg is sufficiently negative, the admetal segregates to the surface to produce the MHH surface monolayer structure. If Eseg is sufficiently positive, the surface layer is the host metal, leading to the formation of the HMH subsurface monolayer structure.\"\n          [5] \"the thermodynamic driving force of hydrogen-induced segregation was related to two terms: the value of Eseg and the difference between the hydrogen-binding energies on the surface and subsurface structures.\"\n          [6] \"Because hydrogen binds relatively weakly to most transition metals, only a few bimetallic surfaces experience a hydrogen-induced segregation of the subsurface admetals.\"\n          [7] \"the subsurface configuration remains thermodynamically preferred with adsorbed hydrogen. However, the subsurface structure is no longer stable in the presence of 0.5 ML atomic oxygen, indicating a thermodynamic driving potential for the subsurface 3d metals to segregate to the surface.\"\n          [8] \"An adsorbate with a weak Pauling electronegativity, such as H, S, Se, or C, binds weakly with both surface and subsurface configurations and provides only a small shift in the thermodynamic potential for interchanging the configuration.\"\n          [9] \"An adsorbate with a strong Pauling electronegativity, such as N or O, produces a large difference in binding energy between the two configurations. When the surface coverage of N or O is high enough, this difference outweighs the thermodynamic potential for retaining the subsurface configuration in vacuum.\"\n          [10] \"The oxygen-induced surface segregation is also predicted for the (211) surfaces of Pt(shell)/M(core) and Pt3M (M = Co, Ir) alloys by Callejas-Tovar and Balbuena.\"", "English": "The stability of bimetallic surface structures under reaction conditions is a critical factor in bimetallic catalysis, as it can modify catalytic properties. For example, under annealing conditions, the subsurface PtNiPt(111) structure is more thermodynamically stable than the surface NiPtPt(111) structure in vacuum. Pt3d alloys in fuel cell applications often have a Pt skin, indicating a thermodynamic preference for Pt-terminated bimetallic surfaces. The segregation energy (Eseg) determines whether the admetal segregates to the surface or remains in the subsurface, affecting the surface composition. Hydrogen-induced segregation is influenced by Eseg and the difference in hydrogen-binding energies on the surface and subsurface. Oxygen can destabilize the subsurface structure, driving subsurface 3d metals to segregate to the surface. Adsorbates with weak Pauling electronegativity have a small effect on the thermodynamic potential for interchanging surface and subsurface configurations, while those with strong Pauling electronegativity can significantly alter binding energy, leading to surface segregation when their coverage is high enough. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Under reductive conditions (vacuum and CO), 3d TM prefers to occupy in the subsurface layer due to its higher surface energy and smaller atomic size.\"\n          [2] \"While under the oxidizing conditions, the strong O3d TMs interaction would induce 3d TM segregation to the surface, migrating further in the surface layer to form oxidized 3d TM islands.\"\n          [3] \"We find that Pt (1 1 1) with the atomic dispersed 3d TM in surface layers is highly active for CO oxidation via bi-functional mechanism.\"\n          [4] \"The interplay between structure and reaction environment as well as effect on the catalytic activity is highlighted.\"", "English": "The thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions significantly influence the stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys. Under reductive conditions, such as vacuum and carbon monoxide, 3d transition metals prefer to occupy the subsurface layer due to their higher surface energy and smaller atomic size. However, under oxidizing conditions, the strong interaction between oxygen and 3d transition metals induces their segregation to the surface, leading to the formation of oxidized 3d transition metal islands. These structural changes have profound implications for the catalytic properties and performance of these materials in various applications. For instance, Pt(111) surfaces with atomic dispersed 3d transition metals in the surface layers exhibit high activity for CO oxidation through a bi-functional mechanism. The interplay between the structure, reaction environment, and catalytic activity is a critical factor in determining the performance of alloy catalysts.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.cattod.2010.10.069"},

in response to the redox potential of the environmental gas. These changes can be controlled by treating the catalysts in various gases. Surface segregation is a common phenomenon in bimetallic surfaces, where a metal with a larger atomic radius tends to segregate to the surface to release strain. Platinum, with its larger atomic radius compared to cobalt, nickel, and copper, is more likely to segregate to the surface. The presence of hydrogen does not alter the thermodynamic driving force for surface segregation. Oxygen adsorption on Pt-M catalyst surfaces can induce the segregation of 3d transition metals to the top surface due to the strong bonding between oxygen and 3d transition metals. In oxidative environments, 3d transition metals can become oxidized, and the formed oxides tend to encapsulate platinum surfaces. The mass transport processes that occur during oxidation or reduction reactions are fully reversible, depending on the redox potential of the reaction gases. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Fe-Pt catalysts have also been used in the preferential oxidation (PROX) of CO in excess H2, and a high selectivity of approximately 100% for CO oxidation was ob- MA Teng et al. / Chinese Journal of Catalysis, 2010, 31: 2432\"\n          [2] \"The Pt-skin structures also showed high activity for catalyzing the hydrogenation of unsaturated hydrocarbons [13].\"\n          [3] \"The unique catalytic properties of the Pt-skin surfaces were attributed to the modification of the electronic structure of the surface Pt by the subsurface Fe underneath, which influenced the adsorption and reaction characteristics of the surface species [14,15].\"\n          [4] \"The controlled preparation of the various Fe-Pt surface structures enabled us to derive the rela- tionship between the structure and catalytic properties, which would help the design of high-performance and low-cost catalysts.\"", "English": "The stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys, are influenced by thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions. Fe-Pt catalysts have been used in the preferential oxidation (PROX) of CO in excess H2, achieving high selectivity for CO oxidation. Pt-skin structures show high activity for catalyzing the hydrogenation of unsaturated hydrocarbons. The unique catalytic properties of the Pt-skin surfaces are attributed to the modification of the electronic structure of the surface Pt by the subsurface Fe underneath, influencing the adsorption and reaction characteristics of the surface species. The controlled preparation of various Fe-Pt surface structures allows for the derivation of the relationship between structure and catalytic properties, aiding in the design of high-performance and low-cost catalysts. The stability of the Pt-skin structure can be reduced under certain circumstances, such as an oxidative environment, leading to gas-induced Fe surface segregation. The structural configuration of the catalyst can be transformed between surface Fe or FeO and subsurface Fe on the Pt(111) surface by cycles of oxidation and reduction. These transformations have implications for the catalytic properties and performance of the catalysts in various applications, including oxygen reduction reactions, hydrogenation of unsaturated hydrocarbons, and the preferential oxidation of CO in excess H2. Depending on the preparation procedures and reaction conditions, Fe overlayers with either metallic or oxidized states may be present as the topmost surface or in the subsurface region of Pt. Different reactions can produce different Fe-Pt surface structural configurations. For oxygen reduction reactions (ORR), Pt-skin structures with Fe or some other transition metals in the subsurface regions showed a higher activity than pure Pt. For instance, Fe49Pt51 alloys with 100% Pt as the topmost layer had 25 times the activity of pure Pt catalysts in ORR. The optimization of the performance of Fe-Pt structures in magnetism and catalysis places a high demand on the design and control of the surface structural configurations and needs information on the stability of the various structures at elevated temperatures and under different reaction atmospheres.", "Doi": "10.1016/s1872-2067(09)60037-1"},

This phenomenon is crucial in materials science, catalysis, and surface chemistry, affecting properties like permeation, surface adsorption, crystal growth, and metal corrosion. Surface segregation can significantly alter the catalytic activities of alloys, as evidenced by enhanced oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) activities in Pt alloys with 3d metals compared to pure Pt. The results showed that after Ru deposition and subsequent re-alloying, the original surface alloy composition was almost completely restored, indicating that the buried Pd and Pt almost completely segregated to the surface layer. There was a slight loss of Pt during the re-alloying step. The slight differences in the stability of the surface alloy may be due to the lower Pd content (44%) in the surface alloy compared to Pt (70%). The preference for surface segregation of a metal atom dissolved in a host metal is related to the difference in surface energies of the two species and the difference in the optimal nearest-neighbor distances of host and guest metal. The surface energies decrease in the order Ru(0001) (1.31.6 eVatom1), Pt(111) (1 eVatom1) and Pd(111) (0.8 eVatom1), which is in good agreement with the observed tendency for segregation of Pt and Pd. The size mismatch of the larger guest metals Pt (0.277 nm) and Pd (0.275 nm) compared to the Ru(0001) single crystal (0.270 nm) would favor the inclusion of the impurity metals in the tensile stressed outermost Ru layer, providing additional driving force for surface segregation. The floating behavior was studied by following the variation in the surface morphology and structure during a series of four preparation steps: deposition of Pt (Pd) on Ru(0001), surface alloy formation by annealing, deposition of Ru on the surface alloy at 500 K, and re-alloying by annealing. The probability that a small amount of Pt (Pd) on Ru(0001) is dissolved in the subsurface region (buried by a layer of substrate material) during surface alloy formation can be estimated by a simple Boltzmann approach. The observation of a distinct bilayer island growth of Ru on the Pt0.70Ru0.30/Ru(0001) surface alloy at 500 K, in contrast to the layer-by-layer Ru growth on Pd0.44Ru0.56/Ru(0001) under similar growth conditions, may be attributed to the lower Pd content, the different 2D distribution of the components in the latter surface alloy with more Ru3 surface sites as compared to Pt0.70Ru0.30/Ru(0001), or to a less pronounced difference between RuRu bonding and RuPd bonding.", "Doi": "10.1002/cphc.201001087"},

"The stability of Pt3dPt subsurface structures in various conditions is a critical factor in their catalytic properties and performance. These structures are desirable in heterogeneous catalysis and electrocatalysis. DFT predictions indicate that these structures are stable in vacuum and with adsorbed hydrogen, but with adsorbed oxygen, the 3d atoms should segregate to the surface. These predictions have been experimentally verified on selected Pt3dPt(111) systems and expanded to polycrystalline Pt to understand the effects of multiple crystal facets on the kinetic barriers for the segregation of subsurface 3d atoms. The Pt3dPt subsurface structures are predicted to have higher activation barriers for oxygen-induced segregation compared to the respective Pt3dPt(111) configuration. The PtNiPt system is the most resistant to oxygen-induced segregation, while the PtTiPt configuration is the most susceptible. The stability of these structures in vacuum and with adsorbed hydrogen and oxygen has been demonstrated through DFT calculations and experimental verification on both single crystal Pt(111) and polycrystalline Pt foil. Using combined theoretical and experimental approaches, the relative stability of surface and subsurface bimetallic structures can be quantified and general trends predicted. The difference in the occupied d-band center, Δεd, can be used to screen potential bimetallic alloys with desirable stability in different environments. A BrnstedEvansPolanyi relationship has been derived to correlate thermodynamic stability from DFT calculations with kinetic barriers from experimental measurements for the Pt3dPt systems. Empirical equations have been developed to predict the thermodynamic stability of other bimetallic systems in various conditions. The large difference in binding energies between surface and subsurface structures highlights the need to understand their stability in reacting environments. For Pt3d bimetallic systems, the difference in oxygen binding energy for these configurations is on the order of hundreds of kcal/mol. Based on Sabatier's principle, if a subsurface alloy structure exhibits optimal binding properties for dehydrogenation and ORR, the surface structure would likely bind intermediates too strongly, halting the reaction. Conversely, if the surface alloy structure exhibits optimal binding properties for steam reforming, the subsurface structure would bind adsorbates too weakly, preventing the reaction from proceeding. The stability of bimetallic structures has been considered with various atomic species, which can be extended to more complex adsorbates using scaling relationships. DFT results predict that subsurface PtMPt(111) configurations are preferred in vacuum and with 0.5 ML H, while surface MPtPt(111) configurations are preferred with 0.5 ML O. Experimental measurements have verified the predicted segregation of subsurface 3d atoms in the presence of oxygen and quantified the activation barrier for adsorbate-induced segregation. AES results suggest two regions of segregation, revealing that DFT predictions and experimental measurements on single crystal bimetallic surfaces are applicable to polycrystalline substrates, bridging the materials gap. Predictions show that all Pt3dPt(100) structures have a higher activation barrier for oxygen-induced segregation compared to respective Pt3dPt(111) configurations. Linear trends in all systems show that the correlation of thermodynamic stability with the difference in d-band center can be used beyond Pt3d alloys. Switching the admetal/host component shifts the absolute driving potential of the segregation. This general stability trend predicts that all bimetallic systems have the same thermodynamically preferred configuration in each environment, with subsurface configurations preferred in vacuum and with 0.5 ML H, and surface configurations favored with 0.5 ML O.", "Doi": "10.1007/s11244-010-9447-2"},

Platinum is a popular cathode electrocatalyst in PEM fuel cells, but its slow kinetics for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) limits catalytic efficiency. In recent years, Pt-based alloys, which include Pt and other transition metals, have been extensively studied due to their enhanced ORR activity. Surface segregation plays a key role in this process, leading to the formation of a pure Pt-skin in the outermost layer of the alloys. This surface segregation is closely related to the enhanced stability of Pt-based catalysts. Au shows strong surface segregation in PtAu alloys, whereas Co is usually unable to enrich PtCo alloy surfaces. The process of Au segregating from the second layer to the surface is exothermic, indicating that Au surface segregation is thermodynamically favorable. However, for the PtCo alloy, the surface Co atoms have a stronger tendency to migrate to the second layer, making the continuous segregation of Co unlikely. Therefore, Co segregating to the clean Pt(111) surface is both thermodynamically unfavorable and kinetically prohibitive. Surface segregation has been found to play a key role in the process of Pt-based alloys. For instance, Stamenkovic et al. found that the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) is uniquely active on Pt-skin surfaces, and the reaction rate is four times that of the pure Pt catalyst. Numerous studies have been carried out using modern experimental and computational techniques to understand the importance of surface segregation for Pt-based alloys. For many Pt alloy systems, segregation trends have been unambiguously identified, and the components of surface and subsurface regions have successfully been characterized. The dissolution of metal atoms results in some defects on the alloy surface, which are critical to the rearrangement process. The phenomenon can also be observed in acidic solution, often with dissolution of the alloyed metal and rearrangement of Pt atoms. The segregation process includes interlayer migration of the impurity atom, vacancy movement at the second layer, and vacancy regeneration at the surface layer. The pathway from 1 to 4 describes a complete cycle in which an impurity atom in the second layer segregates to the Pt(111) surface. {"Quotes": "", "English": "The thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions significantly influence the stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys. The introduction of 3d metals into the Pt(111) system considerably alters the surface adsorption properties, which can be considerably modified by the 3d metals. The electronic structure of the surface Pt atoms only differs very slightly for the segregated and nonsegregated alloys. The discrepant adsorption properties are therefore mainly attributed to the direct involvement of 3d metals either on the surface or in the subsurface. The stability of Pt atoms of Pt-skin surfaces of the three alloys is enhanced with respect to those in pure Pt surfaces. Such stability enhancement is observed both in clean surfaces and under 0.25 ML of adsorbed atomic oxygen, and the degree of stability enhancement follows the opposite order of the oxygen adsorption strengths in the three studied alloys, indicating the protective role of the 3d subsurface atoms determined by their strong binding to the Pt surface layer. The presence of 3d metals in the alloys modifies the electronic and geometric properties, altering the binding strength of adsorbates. This can lead to enhanced catalytic properties and performance in various applications, such as increased oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) rate and improved electrochemical stability against dissolution. The formation of a Pt-skin layer on the surface of Pt-3d alloys, accompanied by a Pt-depleted layer underneath, is a result of surface segregation that can facilitate the removal of adsorbed species, increasing the ORR rate. The stability enhancement of Pt-skin surfaces is associated with a positive potential shift, indicating that the dissolution of Pt atoms is delayed to higher potentials. The presence of adsorbates, such as oxygen, can affect the stability of the catalysts, but Pt-skin surfaces from the alloys still show enhanced stability compared to pure Pt under adsorbate coverage.", "Doi": "10.1021/jp8046888"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"For many alloys, the chemical composition at the surface is significantly different from that in the bulk. In general, one metal species enriches the surface or the first sub-surface layers over other components. The phenomenon called surface segregation has drawn much experimental and theoretical attention due to its importance to materials science and surface chemistry.\"\n          [2] \"It influences many important physical and chemical properties such as permeation, surface adsorption, crystal growth, metal corrosion, etc. Particularly, it has been found that the catalytic activities of alloys can be altered dramatically through surface segregation.\"\n          [3] \"As an efficient catalyst, Pt is used in many important chemical reactions such as methanol oxidation and hydrocarbon reforming. In particular, Pt has become the most widely used electrocatalyst for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) in fuel cells.\"\n          [4] \"For many years, numerous efforts to search alternative catalysts to Pt have mainly focused on Pt-based bimetallic alloys, in which some of the platinum is replaced by less expensive metals.\"\n          [5] \"Since the catalytic properties are chiefly determined by the surface geometry and the electronic structure which can be modified by the chemical composition of the bimetallic alloys, the catalytic effect can be considerably improved when appropriate structures are achieved.\"\n          [6] \"Enhanced ORR activities have been reported in the case of Pt alloyed with 3d metals.\"\n          [7] \"Notably, surface segregation plays a key role in the ORR.\"\n          [8] \"For instance, Stamenkovic et al. compared the kinetics of the ORR on bulk Pt3Co alloy and on the Pt-skin structure produced by Pt segregating on the outmost layer of the annealed alloy surface. In general, one metal species enriches the surface or the first sub-surface layers over other components. The phenomenon called surface segregation has drawn much experimental and theoretical attention due to its importance to materials science and surface chemistry.\"\n          [2] \"It influences many important physical and chemical properties such as permeation, surface adsorption, crystal growth, metal corrosion, etc. Particularly, it has been found that the catalytic activities of alloys can be altered dramatically through surface segregation.\"\n          [3] \"As an efficient catalyst, Pt is used in many important chemical reactions such as methanol oxidation and hydrocarbon reforming. In particular, Pt has become the most widely used electrocatalyst for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) in fuel cells.\"\n          [4] \"For many years, numerous efforts to search alternative catalysts to Pt have mainly focused on Pt-based bimetallic alloys, in which some of the platinum is replaced by less expensive metals.\"\n          [5] \"The catalytic properties are chiefly determined by the surface geometry and the electronic structure which can be modified by the chemical composition of the bimetallic alloys, the catalytic effect can be considerably improved when appropriate structures are achieved.\"\n          [6] \"Enhanced ORR activities have been reported in the case of Pt alloyed with 3d metals.\"\n          [7] \"Notably, surface segregation plays a key role in the ORR.\"\n          [8] \"For instance, Stamenkovic et al. compared the kinetics of the ORR on bulk Pt3Co alloy and on the Pt-skin structure produced by Pt segregating on the outmost layer of the annealed alloy surface. They noted that the ORR is uniquely active on the Pt-skin surface and the reaction rate is 4 times that for the pure Pt catalyst.\"\n          [9] \"The enhanced activities can be attributed to two critical factors: the surface electronic structure and the surface bond length of alloy systems.\"\n          [10] \"The surface geometry has been argued to be able to considerably alter the catalytic efficiency.\"\n          [11] \"For each non-segregated alloy, the surface metal bond length varies narrowly (up to 0.12 Å).\"\n          [12] \"The surface geometry for the Pt-segregated structures is found to be close to the non-segregated geometry, although the M atoms are all replaced by Pt atoms.\"\n          [13] \"For the well-studied Pt3Co (1 1 1) and Pt3Ni (1 1 1) systems, the maximum deviation of the surface bond length is only 0.002 Å after Pt segregation.\"\n          [14] \"Therefore, the geometric effect does not contribute to the enhanced ORR activities of Pt-skin surfaces; instead the electronic structures modified through surface segregation should be the main factor for such enhancement.\"\n          [1] \"For many alloys, the chemical composition at the surface is significantly different from that in the bulk. Surface segregation, where one metal species enriches the surface or the first sub-surface layers over other components, has significant implications for materials science and surface chemistry. It affects physical and chemical properties such as permeation, surface adsorption, crystal growth, and metal corrosion. Notably, the catalytic activities of alloys can be dramatically altered through surface segregation. Pt, an efficient catalyst used in important chemical reactions like methanol oxidation and hydrocarbon reforming, is the most widely used electrocatalyst for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) in fuel cells. Efforts to find alternative catalysts to Pt have focused on Pt-based bimetallic alloys, where some platinum is replaced by less expensive metals. The catalytic properties of these alloys are determined by the surface geometry and electronic structure, which can be modified by the chemical composition, leading to improved catalytic effects when appropriate structures are achieved. Enhanced ORR activities have been observed in Pt alloys with 3d metals, with surface segregation playing a key role. For example, Stamenkovic et al. found that the ORR on Pt-skin structures produced by Pt segregating on the surface of annealed Pt3Co alloys is uniquely active, with a reaction rate four times that of pure Pt. The enhanced activities can be attributed to the surface electronic structure and surface bond length of alloy systems. The surface geometry can considerably alter catalytic efficiency, but for Pt3Co (1 1 1) and Pt3Ni (1 1 1) systems, the maximum deviation of the surface bond length after Pt segregation is only 0.002 Å, indicating that the geometric effect does not contribute to the enhanced ORR activities of Pt-skin surfaces. Instead, the electronic structures modified through surface segregation are the main factor for such enhancement. Theoretical approaches can help to elucidate the driving force and the tendency of this phenomenon. Surface segregation is also governed by temperature and by the presence of adsorbates.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.susc.2007.09.052"},
{"Quotes": "", "English": "The literature does not provide specific information on how thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions influence the stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys, and the consequent implications for their catalytic properties and performance in various applications. However, it does discuss the growth of silver on a Pt(1 0 0) surface and the evolution of the structure with increasing temperature, which can be related to the stability and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts. At room temperature, the formation of elongated 2D-adislands with a composition of Ag80Pt20 was observed. Annealing of the sample above 600 K causes more extensive alloying starting at the perimeter of the Ag adislands by AgPt exchange and formation of a nanophasic AgPt alloy confined to the surface layer. With sufficient Ag, this alloy spreads across the surface until a uniform stoichiometry is established over the entire surface. Temperature-dependent AES studies indicate that there is some mixing of Ag with bulk Pt at elevated temperatures. These findings suggest that the stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts can be influenced by thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions, which in turn can affect their catalytic properties and performance in various applications.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.susc.2003.12.023"},

segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys, are influenced by thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions. Some near-surface alloys (NSAs) bind atomic hydrogen as weakly as noble metals while dissociating H2 more easily, which is beneficial for hydrogen-related reactions. Hydrogen chemistry on NSAs is crucial for many chemical and pharmaceutical processes. Density functional theory calculations are used to develop new NSAs for these processes. Selected NSAs allow weak hydrogen binding and low H2 dissociation barriers, which is an exception to the general rule. The lowering of the transition-state energy is observed even on non-ideal NSAs, although the effect is less pronounced. NSAs promise high hydrogenation activity at low temperatures, which is beneficial for the production of chiral pharmaceutical compounds and could potentially permit easier separation and storage of hydrogen. The stability of near-surface alloys (NSAs) can be assessed using a two-step procedure: first, considering the composition in vacuum, and then accounting for the effects of hydrogen adsorption. Adsorbates can draw to the surface components of alloys to which they bind strongly. The tendency of hydrogen to induce surface segregation can be estimated by comparing the segregation energy (Eseg) of the solute to the difference in the magnitudes of the hydrogen binding energies between the pure solute and the pure host. Real NSAs may differ from idealized models, but these deviations do not substantially affect the main trends in adsorbate behavior. Some experimental studies have shown significantly modified catalytic activity on non-ideal NSAs, in line with theoretical predictions.", "Doi": "10.1038/nmat1223"},
{"Quotes": "", "English": "The thermodynamic preferences and reaction conditions can influence the stability, surface segregation, and structural configuration of bimetallic catalysts, particularly in Pt-based systems such as Pt-3d alloys. The addition of a third transition metal element M can modify the driving energy for Pt segregation to the outermost layer of Pt3Ni (111) surface. Transition metals V, Fe, Co, Mo, Tc, Ru, W, Re, Os, and Ir were predicted to lead to even more negative Pt surface segregation energies than that in the based Pt3Ni (111) surface, further promoting Pt surface segregation. In contrast, doping transition metals Ag, Au, and Pd would lead to positive segregation energies and hence suppress the Pt surface segregation in the corresponding Pt3Ni/M (111) surface. The surface energy effect, strain effect, and heat of solution effect induced by the doped element M would collectively affect the Pt surface segregation energy in the Pt3Ni/M (111) surfaces. The extent of Pt surface segregation is determined by the relative Pt surface segregation energy, lattice mismatch, and heat of solution of the Pt3M crystal with respect to those of the Pt3Ni crystal. Replacing Ni with transition metals V, Fe, Co, Mo, Tc, Ru, W, Re, Os, and Ir would lead to even stronger Pt segregation extent to the surface while replacing Ni with transition metals Ag, Au, and Pd could completely prohibit Pt segregating to the surface.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.susc.2011.05.032"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"The higher stability of clean alloy surfaces can indeed be inferred by the calculated Pt d-band structure, which shows that the alloying generally leads to the stabilization of the Pt d-band,8b,34, and therefore the surface Pt atoms are more difficult to segregate or dissolve.\"\n          [2] \"By contrast, in the presence of adsorbed O atoms the Pt removal is facilitated, because the surface vacancy can be more strongly stabilized by O atoms compared to the initial perfect surface.\"\n          [3] \"This can be understood as follows. activity.\"", "English": "The stability of Pt-based alloys, such as Pt-3d alloys, is influenced by the oxygen coverage under electrochemical conditions. Alloys are generally less stable than pure Pt at the same oxygen coverage. Maintaining zero oxygen coverage at working potentials is crucial for achieving both high activity and stability. The stability of clean alloy surfaces can be inferred from the calculated Pt d-band structure, which shows that alloying generally leads to the stabilization of the Pt d-band, making surface Pt atoms more difficult to segregate or dissolve. However, in the presence of adsorbed O atoms, the removal of Pt is facilitated because the surface vacancy can be more strongly stabilized by O atoms. This is due to the fact that Pt atoms at the surface vacancy are less coordinated and possess more active d-states, which can more strongly bond with O atoms compared to terrace Pt atoms. Additionally, with the exposure of the second layer atoms at the surface vacancy, it is possible to form a much stronger OM bond compared to the OPt bond. This is observed in the search for the optimum structure after a Pt removal, which shows that the surface vacancy is preferentially terminated by O atoms that sink to bond with the solute M atoms at the second layer. For example, in Pt2Ti skin alloys, the surface vacancies with a configuration containing OTi bonding are much more stable than those with only OPt bonding. This is consistent with the fact that atomic O on metals is anion-like, with a strong tendency to accept electrons, and prefers to form ionic-bonds. The stability of Pt alloys under electrochemical conditions is generally lower than that of pure Pt when the oxygen coverage is the same, and maintaining zero oxygen coverage at working potentials (such as 0.9 V) is crucial for achieving both high activity and stability. Among more than 20 different Pt2M alloys, molybdenum (Mo) is identified as a good element that can alloy with Pt to form a stable surface at high electrochemical potentials. Pt2Mo has a lower kinetic barrier for oxygen reduction (OR) compared to Pt, and the barrier to OOH dissociation is a key kinetic parameter for OR activity. The higher stability of clean alloy surfaces can indeed be inferred from the calculated Pt d-band structure, which shows that alloying generally leads to the stabilization of the Pt d-band, making surface Pt atoms more difficult to segregate or dissolve. The stability of surfaces follows the order Pt2Mo > Pt > Pt2Ag > Pt2Pd > Pt2Nb > Pt2Ti > Pt2Sc > Pt2Zr, and only Pt2Mo exhibits higher stability than pure Pt at high potential (1.05 V). The oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) on a Pt2Mo alloy surface follows a similar reaction profile as on Pt, with the transition state (TS) for OOH dissociation determining the height of the potential profile. The TSs for OOH dissociation and O2 dissociation dictate the highest free energy positions in the reaction pathways. The aqueous environment, together with the in situ oxygen coverage, plays an important role in facilitating the ORR on alloy surfaces. The stability of PtxMo alloys drops quickly with increasing potential, in line with the rapid increase of surface oxygen coverage for PtxMo surfaces above 0.9 V. The Pt2Mo skin alloy achieves the best balance of stability and activity. A low Mo content (below 30%, e.g., Pt5Mo) may achieve a higher stability at high potentials, but the activity is not better than Pt at 0.9 V, while a high Mo content (above 30%, e.g., PtMo) will start to destabilize the alloy and also reduce the activity.", "Doi": "10.1039/c0ee00762e"},
